Ali Describes the Special Knowledge of Each Companion
Ibn Asakir reports a hadith and then Ali's evaluations of Companions' knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ali ibn Abi Talib characterized the distinct knowledge of various Companions, highlighting their specializations.

Core Idea
Each Companion had a unique strength in knowledge, not all were the same.

This passage from Ibn Asakir presents a fascinating glimpse into the early Muslim community's recognition of individual expertise among the Companions. Through Ali's responses, we see that each Companion was known for a particular domain of knowledge, from Qur'an and Sunnah to understanding hypocrisy. This helps us appreciate the diversity of talents among the Prophet's companions.

Guided Path
1
Read the hadith and the question session
First, read the full Arabic and translation to get the context: the Prophet's advice to Mu'adh and Abu Musa, then the question to Ali about the Companions.
Pay attention to the chain of narration; it shows the transmission care.
2
Identify each Companion and Ali's description
List each Companion mentioned (Abdullah, Abu Musa, Hudhayfa, Ammar, Abu Dharr, Salman) and note what Ali said about their knowledge.
3
Understand the implications
Reflect on what these descriptions tell us about specialization and the recognition of different types of knowledge among the Sahaba.
This challenges the misconception that all Companions were equally knowledgeable in all fields.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
عنه قال أوصانا رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم حين بعثنا إلى اليمن أنا وبعاداً نعلمهم السنة قال فأوصانا حين أردنا نتوجه قال ( بشروا ولا تنفروا ويسروا ولا تعسروا) في حديث ذكره .
أخبرنا الشيخ أبو القسم إسماعيل بن أحمد بن السمرقندي أنا محمد ابن أحمد بن على بن الحسن بن أبي عثمان وأبوطاهر أحمد بن محمد بن إبراهيم القصارى ح وأخبرنا الشيخ أبو عبد الله محمد بن أحمد ابن القصارى أنا أبى قالا أنا أبو القسم إسماعيل بن الحسن بن عبد الله العسر صرى نا الحسين بن إسماعيل المحاملي نا الحسن بن محمد بن الصباح نا محمد بن عبيد نا الأعمش عن عمرو بن مرة عن أبى البختري قال أتينا علياً رضى الله عنه فسألناه عن أصحاب محمد صلى الله عليه وسلم قال أيهم قلنا عن عبد الله قال ( علم القرآن والسنة ثم انتهى وكفى به علما ) قلنا أبو موسى قال ( صبغ في العلم صبغة ثم خرج منه قلنا حذيفة قال ( اعلم أصحاب محمد صلى الله عليه وسلم بالمنافقين) قلنا عمار قال ) مؤمن نسى إن ذكرته ذكر ) قلنا أبو ذر قال ( وعى علماً ثم عجز فيه ) قلنا سلمان قال ( أدرك العلم الأول والآخر بحر لا يدرك قعره منا أهل البيت ) قلنا أخبرنا عن نفسك يا أمير المؤمنين قال ( كنت إذا سألت أعطيت وإذا سكنت انتدبت)
He narrated from him: The Messenger of Allah, peace be upon him, advised us when he sent us to Yemen, myself and Mu'adh, to teach them the Sunnah. He advised us when we were about to depart: 'Give glad tidings and do not cause aversion, and make things easy and do not make them difficult' in a hadith he mentioned.
Shaykh Abu al-Qasim Ismail ibn Ahmad al-Samarqandi informed us, Muhammad ibn Ahmad ibn Ali ibn al-Hasan ibn Abi Uthman and Abu Tahir Ahmad ibn Muhammad ibn Ibrahim al-Qasari informed us, and Shaykh Abu Abdullah Muhammad ibn Ahmad ibn al-Qasari informed us, my father informed us, they said: Abu al-Qasim Ismail ibn al-Hasan ibn Abdullah al-Askari [؟] informed us, al-Husayn ibn Ismail al-Mahamili narrated to us, al-Hasan ibn Muhammad ibn al-Sabbah narrated to us, Muhammad ibn Ubayd narrated to us, al-A'mash narrated to us from Amr ibn Murra from Abu al-Bakhtari who said: We came to Ali, may Allah be pleased with him, and asked him about the companions of Muhammad, peace be upon him. He said: 'Which of them?' We said: 'Abdullah.' He said: 'He knew the Qur'an and the Sunnah, then he stopped, and that was sufficient knowledge.' We said: 'Abu Musa.' He said: 'He was dyed in knowledge with a dye, then he came out of it.' We said: 'Hudhayfa.' He said: 'The most knowledgeable of the companions of Muhammad, peace be upon him, about the hypocrites.' We said: 'Ammar.' He said: 'A believer who forgot; if you remind him, he remembers.' We said: 'Abu Dharr.' He said: 'He acquired knowledge, then became incapable of it.' We said: 'Salman.' He said: 'He attained the first and last knowledge; a sea whose depth is not reached; he is from us, the People of the House.' We said: 'Tell us about yourself, O Commander of the Faithful.' He said: 'When I was asked, I was given; and when I was silent, I was prompted.'
Source pages p. 73

Commentary
This passage is a report of a hadith and then a question-and-answer session where Ali ibn Abi Talib describes the specific knowledge of several Companions. It begins with the Prophet's general instruction to Mu'adh and Abu Musa to be lenient. Then, through a chain of transmission, Abu al-Bakhtari narrates that he and others came to Ali and asked about various Companions. For each, Ali gives a distinct characterization: Abdullah ibn Mas'ud mastered the Qur'an and Sunnah and then stopped; Abu Musa was immersed in knowledge but later left it; Hudhayfa specialized in knowing the hypocrites; Ammar was a believer who sometimes forgot; Abu Dharr learned much but could not act on it fully; Salman possessed comprehensive knowledge like a bottomless sea and was from the Prophet's household; and Ali himself said that when asked he was given knowledge, and when silent he was inspired. This shows the early recognition of specialization among the Companions.

Key Terms
أصحاب محمد
علم القرآن والسنة
المنافقين
أهل البيت

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That all Companions were equally knowledgeable in all fields.
Correction: Ali's descriptions show each Companion had a specific strength: Abdullah ibn Mas'ud in Qur'an and Sunnah, Hudhayfa in hypocrisy, etc.

Checkpoint
According to Ali, which Companion was most knowledgeable about the hypocrites?
A) Abdullah ibn Mas'ud
B) Hudhayfa ibn al-Yaman
C) Abu Musa al-Ash'ari
D) Salman al-Farisi

Key Takeaway
Ali's descriptions reveal that the Companions had distinct specializations, and no single companion encompassed all knowledge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When Ali said about himself, 'When I was asked, I was given; and when I was silent, I was prompted,' do you recognize that same posture of humility and reliance on Allah's grace in your own pursuit of knowledge? What would change in your study habits if you truly believed knowledge is a divine gift, not a personal achievement?
?
The Companions each had a unique strength and acknowledged limitations. Are you tempted to compare your own knowledge to others and feel inadequate, or to dismiss your own specialization as insignificant? How can you, like Ali, value the depth and purpose of the knowledge Allah has given you, even if it seems narrow?
?
Salman was described as a sea of knowledge and included in Ahl al-Bayt. How does your connection to the Prophet's family (through love and following) shape your pursuit of knowledge? Are you actively seeking knowledge that brings you closer to them, or are you satisfied with a superficial understanding?

The Four Judges and Six Muftis Among the Companions
Ali ibn al-Madini reports the four judges and six muftis from the Companions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the early Islamic tradition recognizing specific Companions as the primary judges and muftis.

Core Idea
A limited group of Companions were recognized as the chief judges and muftis.

This lesson examines a report from the famous hadith critic Ali ibn al-Madini, which identifies the Companions who served as judges and those who issued fatwas. The report gives us insight into how the early Muslim community viewed legal authority.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the chain of transmission and the lists come through.
Notice the chain of narrators leading to Ali ibn al-Madini.
2
Identify the two lists
The passage contains two lists: four judges and six muftis. Underline the names in each list and note who appears in both.
Zayd ibn Thabit appears in both lists.
3
Reflect on the significance
Consider why only four judges and six muftis were singled out. What does this tell us about the early Islamic legal system?
This shows an early consensus on legal authority.
4
Answer the checkpoint
Test your understanding by answering the multiple-choice question based solely on this passage.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أخبرنا الشيخ أبو عبد الله محمد بن الفضل أنا أبوبكر أحمد بن الحسين الحافظ أنا محمد بن عبدالله الحافظ أنا الحسين بن محمد ابن اسحق أنا محمد بن أحمد بن البراء قال سمعت على بن عبد الله المدينى يقول : كان يقال قضاة هذه الأمة أربعة عمر بن الخطاب وعلى بن أبي طالب وزيد بن ثابت وأبو موسى الأشعرى رضى الله عنهم قال على: و كان الفتيا في أصحاب رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم في ستة : عمر وعلى وعبد الله وزيد وأبى موسى وأبي بن كعب رضى الله عنهم
Shaykh Abu Abdullah Muhammad ibn al-Fadl informed us, Abubakr Ahmad ibn al-Husayn al-Hafiz informed us, Muhammad ibn Abdullah al-Hafiz informed us, al-Husayn ibn Muhammad ibn Ishaq informed us, Muhammad ibn Ahmad ibn al-Bara' informed us, he said: I heard Ali ibn Abdullah al-Madini say: It used to be said: The judges of this nation are four: Umar ibn al-Khattab, Ali ibn Abi Talib, Zayd ibn Thabit, and Abu Musa al-Ash'ari, may Allah be pleased with them. Ali said: And legal opinion (fatwa) among the companions of the Messenger of Allah, peace be upon him, was in six: Umar, Ali, Abdullah, Zayd, Abu Musa, and Ubayy ibn Ka'b, may Allah be pleased with them.
Source pages p. 73

Commentary
This passage is a report from the renowned scholar Ali ibn al-Madini, transmitted through a chain of authorities. It contains two lists. First, the four Companions who served as judges (qudat) in the early community: Umar ibn al-Khattab, Ali ibn Abi Talib, Zayd ibn Thabit, and Abu Musa al-Ash'ari. Second, the six Companions who were qualified to issue legal opinions (fatwa): Umar, Ali, Abdullah (Ibn Mas'ud), Zayd, Abu Musa, and Ubayy ibn Ka'b. The passage indicates an early consensus on the leading jurists among the Companions.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Many Companions were qualified to judge.
Correction: This tradition limits the judges to four specific Companions.

B) Zayd ibn Thabit
C) Abdullah ibn Mas'ud
D) Abu Musa al-Ash'ari

Key Takeaway
The early Muslim community recognized a select group of Companions as the primary sources of judicial and legal authority.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

The passage lists Umar, Ali, Zayd, Abu Musa, Abdullah, and Ubayy as the primary sources of legal authority. How does this shape your trust in the transmission of Islamic law? What steps can you take to learn from their legacy and apply it to your contemporary challenges?

Core Idea
Six Companions were the recognized judges among the Sahaba.

This lesson presents a report from the renowned tabi‘i Masruq, transmitted by al-Sha'bi, listing the Companions who served as judges and from whom knowledge was taken. This list confirms a tradition of juristic authority among the early Muslim community.

The Two Schools of Knowledge among the Companions
Sha'bi reports the six from whom knowledge was taken, grouping them into two schools.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how early Muslim scholars classified the Companions into two groups based on the similarity of their knowledge and mutual learning.

Core Idea
The six Companions formed two schools: Umar, Ibn Mas'ud, Zayd; and Ali, Ubayy, al-Ash'ari.

Al-Sha'bi, a prominent tabi'i, reports that knowledge was taken from six Companions. He groups them into two sets whose knowledge resembled each other and who learned from one another. This passage provides insight into the early transmission of Islamic knowledge and the recognition of different scholarly traditions among the Companions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the chain of narration and the names of the Companions.
The chain includes several transmitters; focus on the report content.
2
Identify the two groups
The passage divides the six Companions into two groups of three. Find each group and note the names.
Umar, Abdullah (Ibn Mas'ud), Zayd (ibn Thabit) in one; Ali, Ubayy (ibn Ka'b), and al-Ash'ari (Abu Musa) in the other.
3
Reflect on the significance
Ask yourself why Sha'bi made this distinction. The commentary explains that this shows an early categorization of legal schools.
Consider how this relates to the development of Islamic jurisprudence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أخبرنا الشيخان ابو عبد الله يحيى بن الحسن بن أحمد بن البناء وابو القسم بن السمرقندى قالا انا ابو محمد عبد الله بن محمد الخطيب انا ابو حفص عمر بن ابراهيم بن أحمد بن كثير الکنانی نا عبدالله بن عبد العزيز نا ابو خيثمة نا عباد بن العوام عن الشيبانی یعنی ابا اسحق سليمان بن فيروز عن الشعبي قال : كان يؤخذ العلم عن ستة من رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم فكان عمر وعبد الله وزيد يشبه علمهم بعضهم بعضا وكان يقتبس بعضهم من بعض وكان على وأبى والأشعري يشبه علمهم بعضهم بعضا ويقتبس بعضهم من بعض قال فقلت له : وكان الأشعرى إلى هؤلاء قال كان أحد الفقهاء .
The two shaykhs, Abu Abdullah Yahya ibn al-Hasan ibn Ahmad ibn al-Banna' and Abu al-Qasim ibn al-Samarqandi, informed us, they said: Abu Muhammad Abdullah ibn Muhammad al-Khatib informed us, Abu Hafs Umar ibn Ibrahim ibn Ahmad ibn Kathir al-Kinani informed us, Abdullah ibn Abd al-Aziz narrated to us, Abu Khaythama narrated to us, Abbad ibn al-Awwam narrated to us from al-Shaybani, meaning Abu Ishaq Sulayman ibn Firuz, from al-Sha'bi who said: Knowledge was taken from six from the Messenger of Allah, peace be upon him. Umar, Abdullah, and Zayd had knowledge that resembled each other, and they would learn from one another. Ali, Ubayy, and al-Ash'ari had knowledge that resembled each other, and they would learn from one another. He said: I said to him: 'And was al-Ash'ari one of these?' He said: 'He was one of the jurists.'
Source pages p. 74

Commentary
This passage is a report from al-Sha'bi through a chain including al-Shaybani. It gives a more detailed description of the six Companions from whom knowledge was taken. It divides them into two groups of three: the first group is Umar, Abdullah ibn Mas'ud, and Zayd ibn Thabit, whose knowledge resembled each other and who learned from each other. The second group is Ali, Ubayy ibn Ka'b, and Abu Musa al-Ash'ari, also with similar knowledge and mutual learning. The narrator asked Sha'bi whether Abu Musa was counted among these, and Sha'bi affirmed he was one of the jurists (fuqaha'). This shows an early scholarly categorization of the Companions' legal schools.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That al-Ash'ari was not considered a major jurist.
Correction: Sha'bi confirms he was one of the fuqaha', i.e., a jurist.

Checkpoint

B) The second group
C) Neither group
D) All six were in one group

Key Takeaway
Al-Sha'bi grouped the six Companions into two schools of knowledge, showing an early recognition of different scholarly traditions among the Sahaba.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have just learned that knowledge was taken from six specific companions. Does your own quest for knowledge connect you to a reliable chain of transmission? Which living scholars do you learn from, and how are they connected to the Salaf? Are you actively taking knowledge, or merely collecting information?
?
The companions within each group had knowledge that resembled each other. Does the knowledge you seek align with the understanding of the early Muslims? Do you ever check your beliefs against the consensus of the Salaf?
?
Sha'bi affirmed that Abu Musa al-Ash'ari was a jurist, despite not being as famous as others. Do you respect and benefit from less prominent scholars? Are you selective in whose knowledge you accept based on fame, or do you seek truth wherever it is authentic? Take a moment to evaluate your attitude towards scholars of the past and present.

Who gave fatwa in the Prophet's mosque
Safwan ibn Sulaym reports the muftis in the Prophet's mosque during his lifetime.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand which Companions were authorized to give fatwa in the Prophet's mosque during his lifetime and the significance of this limitation.

Core Idea
Only four Companions gave fatwa in the Prophet's mosque.

This passage from Safwan ibn Sulaym provides a specific historical detail about legal authority in early Islam. Understanding who gave fatwa in the Prophet's mosque helps us appreciate the careful transmission of religious knowledge. Let's examine the report and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice the chain of transmission and the final statement.
The chain shows the care in preserving this information.
2
Identify the four names
List the four Companions mentioned as muftis. They are Umar, Ali, Mu'adh, and Abu Musa.
These names are well-known scholars among the Sahaba.
3
Understand the chain of transmission
Trace the chain from Safwan ibn Sulaym back to the reporter. Note that multiple narrators are involved, indicating a reliable transmission.
The chain is: Safwan from Usama from Hatim from Sa'id from Muhammad from Muhammad from Abu Nu'aym from the shaykhs.
4
Reflect on the significance
Ask yourself: Why might only these four have given fatwa? This suggests that giving fatwa was a serious responsibility restricted to those with deep knowledge.
This report corrects the misconception that many Companions freely gave legal opinions.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Shaykh Abu Ali al-Hasan ibn Ahmad ibn al-Hasan al-Muqri' informed us in his book to me from Isfahan, and Shaykh Abu Mas'ud Abd al-Rahim ibn Ali ibn Hamd al-Mu'addil narrated to me in Isfahan from him, Abu Nu'aym Ahmad ibn Abdullah ibn Ahmad al-Hafiz informed us, Muhammad ibn Ahmad ibn al-Hasan narrated to us, Muhammad ibn Uthman ibn Abi Shayba narrated to us, Sa'id ibn Amr, who is al-Ash'athi, narrated to us, Hatim ibn Isma'il narrated to us from Usama ibn Zayd from Safwan ibn Sulaym who said: No one used to give fatwa in the mosque of the Messenger of Allah, peace be upon him, during the time of the Messenger of Allah, except these people: Umar, Ali, Mu'adh, and Abu Musa, may Allah be pleased with them.

This passage is a report from Safwan ibn Sulaym, transmitted through a chain, stating that only four Companions issued legal opinions (fatwa) in the Prophet's mosque during his lifetime: Umar ibn al-Khattab, Ali ibn Abi Talib, Mu'adh ibn Jabal, and Abu Musa al-Ash'ari. This is a more restricted list than other reports, emphasizing the special authority of these four in the prophetic era.

Abu Musa's Mission to Basra
Abu Musa al-Ash'ari states his mission from Umar to teach the Book and Sunnah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the comprehensive nature of Abu Musa's mission from Umar, combining religious teaching with public service.

Core Idea
Umar's mission for Abu Musa blended Qur'an, Sunnah, and civic cleaning.

This passage preserves Abu Musa's own account of his appointment to Basra by Caliph Umar. It reveals that the role of a governor-teacher in early Islam was not limited to spiritual instruction but included practical community care. The specific mention of cleaning streets may surprise modern readers, but it underscores the holistic vision of Islamic leadership.

Guided Path
1
Read the chain and statement
Notice the long chain of transmission, then Abu Musa's direct words.
2
Identify the three tasks
Find the three things Umar sent Abu Musa to do: teach the Book, teach the Sunnah, and clean streets.
The third task is often overlooked.
3
Reflect on the combination
Consider why these three tasks were mentioned together—what does it say about Umar's priorities?
4
Connect to your own learning
How does this report inform your understanding of the role of knowledge in early Islam?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أخبرنا الشيوخ أبو الفضل محمد بن اسماعيل بن الفضيل الفضيلي وأبو المحاسن أسعد بن على بن الموفق بن زياد الحنفى وأبو الوقت عبد الأول بن عيسى بن شعيب السجزى وأبو بكر احمد بن يحيى ابن الحسن الاذربيجاني بهراة قالوا انا أبو الحسن عبدالرحمن بن محمد بن المظفر الداودي ببوشنج، أنا أبو محمد عبدالله بن احمد بن حمويه السرخسى انا أبو عمران عيسى بن عمر بن العباس السمرقندى انا أبو محمد عبد الله بن عبدالرحمن الدارمی نا عبيد بن يعيش نا يونس عن صالح بن رستم المزنى عن الحسن عن أبي موسى انه قال حين قدم البصرة : بعثني اليكم عمر بن الله الخطاب رضى عنه اعلمكم كتاب ربكم وسنة نبيكم صلى الله عليه وسلم وأنظف طزقكم
The shaykhs Abu al-Fadl Muhammad ibn Isma'il ibn al-Fudayl al-Fudayli, Abu al-Mahasin As'ad ibn Ali ibn al-Muwaffaq ibn Ziyad al-Hanafi, Abu al-Waqt Abd al-Awwal ibn Isa ibn Shu'ayb al-Sijzi, and Abu Bakr Ahmad ibn Yahya ibn al-Hasan al-Adharbayjani in Herat informed us, they said: Abu al-Hasan Abd al-Rahman ibn Muhammad ibn al-Muzaffar al-Dawudi in Bushanj informed us, Abu Muhammad Abdullah ibn Ahmad ibn Hamawayh al-Sarakhsi informed us, Abu Imran Isa ibn Umar ibn al-Abbas al-Samarqandi informed us, Abu Muhammad Abdullah ibn Abd al-Rahman al-Darimi informed us, Ubayd ibn Ya'ish narrated to us, Yunus narrated to us from Salih ibn Rustam al-Muzani from al-Hasan from Abu Musa that he said when he arrived in Basra: 'Umar ibn al-Khattab, may Allah be pleased with him, sent me to you to teach you the Book of your Lord and the Sunnah of your Prophet, peace be upon him, and to clean your streets.'
Source pages p. 74

Commentary
This passage is a report from Abu Musa al-Ash'ari himself, transmitted through a chain, describing his official mission to Basra. He states that Umar ibn al-Khattab sent him there with three tasks: to teach the Qur'an, to teach the Sunnah of the Prophet, and to clean the streets. This shows the comprehensive role of the early Muslim governor/teacher, combining religious education with public service. The mention of cleaning streets is unusual and highlights the practical dimension of his mission.

?

Umar's Strategic Appointment of Abu Musa to Basra
Ibn Asakir narrates Umar's conversation with Abu Musa about sending him to Basra.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Umar selected leaders based on merit and the significance of jihad and ribat in early Islamic governance.

Core Idea
Umar sent Abu Musa to Basra because of the spiritual duty of jihad and ribat.

In this lesson, we read a brief exchange between Umar and Abu Musa, showing how Umar urged Abu Musa to accept a posting to Basra by highlighting the religious rewards of jihad and ribat. This illustrates Umar's leadership style and the early Islamic concept of frontier defense.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation to understand the exchange.
The passage includes a chain of narration.
2
Notice Umar's initial statement
Umar says that in Syria there are forty men, any of whom would be sufficient if put in charge. This shows the high caliber of the Companions in Syria.
3
Observe Abu Musa's reluctance and Umar's persuasion
Abu Musa initially asks not to be sent, but Umar convinces him by mentioning jihad and ribat. Note how the religious duty overcomes personal reluctance.
This highlights the importance of intention in Islamic actions.
4
Reflect on the terms jihad and ribat
Jihad here refers to defensive struggle, and ribat to frontier guarding. Both carry great spiritual reward, which is why Abu Musa agreed to go.
This context helps understand early Islamic expansion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قرأت على الشيخ أبي غالب احمد بن الحسن المقرى عن أبي اسحق ابراهيم بن عمر البرمكى الفقيه انا أبو عمر محمد بن العباس الخزار أنا أبو الحسن احمد بن معروف الخشاب نا الحسين بن الفهم نا محمد ابن سعد انا عارم بن الفضل تا حماد بن زيد عن ايوب عن محمد قال قال عمر بن الخطاب رضى الله عنه : بالشام اربعون رجلا مامنهم رجل كان يلى أسر الأمة إلا اجزاه فأرسل اليهم فجاء رهط منهم فيهم أبو موسى الأشعرى الله عنه فقال أني أرسلت اليكم لأرسلك إلى قوم عسكر الشيطان بين اظهرهم قال فلا ترسلنى فقال : ان بها جهادا وان بها رباطا قال فأرسله إلى البصرة،
I read to the shaykh... from Muhammad who said: Umar ibn al-Khattab, may Allah be pleased with him, said: In Syria there are forty men, none of whom, if he were put in charge of the affairs of the community, would be inadequate. So he sent for them, and a group came including Abu Musa al-Ash'ari, may Allah be pleased with him. He (Umar) said: I sent for you to send you to a people among whom the devil has camped. He (Abu Musa) said: Do not send me. He (Umar) said: Indeed, there is jihad there and there is ribat (frontier guard). He said: So he sent him to Basra.
Source pages p. 74

Commentary
This passage reports a conversation between Umar and a group of qualified men from Syria, among whom was Abu Musa. Umar initially wanted to send one of them to a place where the devil had influence (perhaps meaning a rebellious or corrupt area). Abu Musa declined, but Umar persuaded him by noting that the place involved jihad and ribat (frontier duty). Ultimately, Umar sent him to Basra. The passage illustrates Umar's strategic assignment of capable men and Abu Musa's reluctance, overcome by the merit of jihad. It also shows that Basra was considered a frontier where ribat was practiced, highlighting its strategic importance in early Islamic expansion.

الشام
أسر الأمة
عسكر الشيطان
جهاد
رباط

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think Abu Musa was eager to go.
Correction: Abu Musa initially asked not to be sent; he only agreed due to the merit of jihad and ribat.

Checkpoint

A) Because Abu Musa volunteered
B) Because Basra had the devil's camp
C) Because there was jihad and ribat in Basra
D) Because the people of Basra requested him

Key Takeaway
Umar sent Abu Musa to Basra by emphasizing the spiritual merits of jihad and ribat.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever avoided a difficult task that had clear spiritual benefits? What held you back, and what would it take for you to embrace a 'jihad and ribat' situation in your own life?
?
Where in your life has the 'devil camped' – a place of moral temptation or corruption – that you are avoiding confronting? Will you go there for the sake of Allah, trusting in the reward of struggle?
?
The forty men of Syria were so capable that any of them could lead the community. How are you preparing yourself to be trustworthy and competent when called upon? What is one step you will take this week to become more reliable for the sake of the ummah?

Abu Musa's Fasting: Training for the Hereafter
Ibn Asakir transmits Abu Musa's analogy comparing fasting to training a racehorse.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the spiritual motivation behind Abu Musa's extreme fasting as expressed through his analogy of the racehorse.

Core Idea
Extreme fasting can be training for the soul, like keeping a racehorse lean.

This passage presents a striking image of Abu Musa's asceticism. When advised to rest his body, he responds with a profound analogy that reveals the inner purpose of his discipline. Let's read the report and unpack its meaning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic text and its literal translation. Pay attention to the chain of transmission and the key details of the story.
The chain is long but establishes authenticity.
2
Understand the analogy
Focus on Abu Musa's response. Why does he compare his fasting to a racehorse that is kept lean? Reflect on the concept of 'idjmam' (rest) and 'tadmir' (training).
The analogy is central to the lesson.
3
Reflect on the broader significance
Consider how this story illustrates a principle of Islamic asceticism: discipline for a higher purpose. How does this relate to the concept of niyyah (intention)?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أخبرنا الشيخ أبو القسم اسماعيل بن احمد انا أبو القسم على بن احمد بن البسرى وأبو محمد احمد بن على بن الحسن الهمذاني وأبو طاهر احمد ابن محمد بن ابراهيم الخوارزمى ببغداد وأخبرنا الشيخ أبو عبدالله محمد ابن أبى طاهر القصارى انا أبى قالوا انا اسماعيل بن الحسن بن عبد الله الصرصري نا أبو عيسى احمد بن اسحق بن عبد الله الانماطی املاء نا العباس ابن عبد الله یعنی الثرقفی نامحمد بن كثير عن أبى المعلى البيروتي عن ابن حلبس وهو يونس بن ميسرة عن أبى ادريس عائذ الله قال صام أبو موسى الأشعرى رضى الله عنه حتى عاد كأنه خلال قال قيل له يا ابا موسى لو اجممت نفسك قال : اجمامها أريد انى رأيت السابق من الخيل المضمر،
The shaykh informed us... from Abu Idris A'idh Allah who said: Abu Musa al-Ash'ari, may Allah be pleased with him, fasted until he became like a broken twig. It was said to him: O Abu Musa, if only you would give your body rest. He said: I intend its rest (by this) – indeed, I saw that the racehorse that is kept lean (trained) is the one that wins the race.
Source pages p. 74

Commentary
This passage is a report about Abu Musa's ascetic practice of frequent fasting. Through a chain, Abu Idris narrates that Abu Musa fasted so much that his body became thin like a 'khilal' (a thin piece of wood or a twig). When advised to rest his body, he explained that his fasting was a form of discipline akin to training a racehorse. Just as a racehorse is kept lean and fit (مضمر) to win the race, Abu Musa's voluntary fasting was intended to train his soul for the afterlife. The analogy shows his deep spiritual motivation and his understanding of asceticism as a means to spiritual excellence, not as an end in itself.

Key Terms
صام
خلال
أجم
السابق من الخيل المضمر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Extreme fasting is always discouraged or harmful.
Correction: Abu Musa's analogy shows that extreme fasting, when done with the correct intention of spiritual training, can be meritorious. It is not self-harm but disciplined preparation for the afterlife.

Checkpoint

A) A bird flying
B) A racehorse kept lean for a race
C) A river flowing
D) A mountain standing firm

Key Takeaway
Abu Musa's fasting was a disciplined training for the afterlife, not mere self-deprivation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You often choose comfort over discomfort. What race are you training for, and when you face hunger or fatigue, do you complain or see it as spiritual conditioning?
?
Your body is like a horse. Are you feeding it for today's pleasure or training it for the eternal finish line? What one sacrifice can you make this week to prove your intent?
?
When someone tells you to 'take it easy' on acts of worship, do you feel justified in slacking off, or do you, like Abu Musa, hold fast to your discipline knowing the real rest is later?

Abu Burda: Name, Judgeship, and Scholarly Status
Ibn Asakir narrates the biography of Abu Burda with chains establishing his name, judgeship, and reliability.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the biographical details of Abu Burda ibn Abi Musa al-Ash'ari, including his name, his tenure as judge of Kufa, and his trustworthiness as a narrator.

Core Idea
Abu Burda was a trustworthy Kufan judge, son of a famous companion, whose name scholars agreed upon but with a rare variant.

Ibn Asakir begins the biography of Abu Burda by establishing his identity and his role as judge of Kufa. Through multiple chains, we learn his name, his dismissal by al-Hajjaj, and his age. This lesson lays the groundwork for the more dramatic story that follows.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage to identify facts
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Note the key facts: his name, his father, his judgeship in Kufa, and his dismissal by Hajjaj.
Pay attention to the chains of narrators; they show the reliability of the reports.
2
Examine the name controversy
Notice that most scholars agree on 'Amir, but Yahya ibn Ma'in reports a rare view that it could be al-Harith. Understand how scholars handle such differences.
3
Reflect on his trustworthiness
The passage explicitly describes him as 'thiqah' (trustworthy). Consider why this matters for the study of hadith and Islamic history.
His trustworthiness is affirmed by al-Ijli.

وانا اشهد ايها الامير اني لست بأهل لما دعوتني اليه فقال له يزيد مازدت على ان حرضتنى على نفسك ورغبنا فيك فاخرج الى عهدك فاني غير معفيك فخرج ثم اقام فيه ماشاء الله ان يقيم فاستأذنه بالقدوم عليه فأذن له الأمير من عملك ) فأعفاه .
As for the mention of his son Abu Burda, his name and virtue, part of it is what the two shaykhs informed us: Abu al-Hasan Ali ibn Hibbat Allah ibn Abd al-Salam al-Katib al-Baghdadi and Abu al-Qasim ibn al-Samarqandi, they said: Abu Bakr ibn Abdullah ibn Muhammad al-Sarifini narrated to us: Ubayd Allah ibn Muhammad ibn Ishaq al-Bazzaz narrated to us: Abdullah ibn Muhammad al-Baghawi narrated to us, he said: The name of Abu Burda is 'Amir ibn Abdullah ibn Qays. Mahmud ibn Ghaylan said that. Also, Salih ibn Ahmad (meaning ibn Hanbal) narrated to me from his father. And the shaykh Abu Abdullah al-Farawi informed us: Abu Bakr al-Bayhaqi narrated to us: Muhammad ibn Ibrahim narrated to us: Ibrahim ibn Abdullah narrated to us: Muhammad ibn Sulayman ibn Faris narrated to us: Muhammad ibn Isma'il al-Bukhari narrated to us, he said: 'Amir ibn Abdullah ibn Qays is Abu Burda ibn Abi Musa al-Ash'ari, may Allah be pleased with him. Amr ibn Ali narrated to me from Abu Dawud from Sulayman ibn Mu'adh from Abu Ishaq, he said: Abu Burda ibn Abi Musa was in charge of the judiciary of Kufa, then al-Hajjaj dismissed him and appointed his brother in his place. He heard from his father, Ali, and Ibn Umar. Ali said: I heard Sufyan say: Umar ibn Abd al-Aziz said to Abu Burda: How old are you? He said: A shaddan, meaning eighty years. And Abu Bakr Muhammad ibn al-Abbas al-Shiqani informed us: Abu Bakr Ahmad ibn Mansur ibn Khalaf al-Qayrawani narrated to us: Abu Sa'id Muhammad ibn Abdullah ibn Hamdawayh narrated to us: Abu Hatim Makki ibn Abdan informed us, he said: I heard Abu al-Husayn Muslim ibn al-Hajjaj al-Qushayri al-Hafiz say: Abu Burda ibn Abi Musa al-Ash'ari is 'Amir ibn Abdullah ibn Qays. He heard from his father and Ali. Al-Sha'bi and Abu Ishaq narrated from him. Most of the memorizers agreed that the name of Abu Burda is 'Amir. Yahya ibn Ma'in said a rare statement about his name. The shaykh Abu Abdullah Muhammad ibn al-Fadl informed us: Ahmad ibn al-Husayn al-Hafiz narrated to us: Abdullah ibn Yahya ibn Abd al-Jabbar al-Sukkari in Baghdad narrated to us: Abu Bakr al-Shafi'i narrated to us: Ja'far ibn Muhammad ibn al-Azhar narrated to us: al-Mufaddal ibn Ghassan al-Ghalabi narrated to us from someone who said: Abu Burda ibn Abi Musa's name is al-Harith. And Abbas ibn Muhammad al-Dawri reported from Yahya ibn Ma'in that he named him with both names, and Abbas mentioned him in his history in two places. And the shaykh Abu al-Fadl Muhammad ibn Isma'il al-Fadili al-Hirawi informed us: Abu al-Qasim Ahmad ibn Muhammad ibn Muhammad al-Khalili in Balkh narrated to us: Abu al-Qasim Ali ibn Ahmad ibn Muhammad ibn al-Hasan al-Khuza'i narrated to us: Abu Sa'id al-Haytham ibn Kulayb al-Shashi narrated to us: Abu Qilaba Abd al-Malik ibn Muhammad al-Raqqashi narrated to us: Raja' ibn Salama ibn Raja narrated to me: my father narrated to me: Qays ibn al-Rabi' narrated to us from Abu Husayn, he said: When al-Hajjaj came to Iraq, he appointed Abd al-Rahman ibn Abi Layla as judge. He said: Then he dismissed him and appointed Abu Burda ibn Abi Musa, and seated Sa'id ibn Jubayr with him. The shaykh Abu al-Barakat Abd al-Wahhab ibn al-Mubarak al-Anmati informed us: Abu al-Husayn al-Mubarak ibn Abd al-Jabbar ibn Ahmad narrated to us: Abu Abdullah al-Husayn ibn Ja'far ibn Muhammad al-Salamsi and his cousin Abu Nasr Muhammad ibn al-Hasan ibn Muhammad [said?] – and the shaykh Abu Abdullah al-Husayn ibn Muhammad ibn Khusraw al-Balkhi in Baghdad informed us: Abu al-Ma'ali Thabit ibn Bundar ibn Ibrahim narrated to us: al-Husayn ibn Ja'far al-Salamsi narrated to us, they said: al-Walid ibn Bakr al-Andalusi narrated to us: Ali ibn Ahmad ibn Zakariyya al-Hashimi narrated to us: Abu Muslim Salih ibn Abdullah ibn Salih al-Ijli narrated to us, he said: My father said: Abu Burda ibn Abi Musa al-Ash'ari is a Kufan, trustworthy, and he was in charge of the judiciary of Kufa after Shurayh, and his scribe was Sa'id ibn Jubayr. The shaykh Abu Sahl Muhammad ibn Ibrahim ibn Muhammad al-Isbahani informed us: Abu al-Fadl Abd al-Rahman ibn Ahmad ibn al-Hasan al-Razi narrated to us: Ja'far ibn Abdullah ibn Ya'qub narrated to us: Muhammad ibn Harun al-Ruwyani narrated to us: Ahmad ibn Abd al-Rahman narrated to us: my uncle, meaning Abdullah ibn Wahab, told me: Abdullah ibn Ayyash narrated to me from his father that when Yazid ibn al-Muhallab became governor of Khurasan, he said: Direct me to a man complete in good qualities. So he was directed to Abu Burda ibn Abi Musa al-Ash'ari. When he came to him, he saw an excellent man. When he spoke to him, he found his inner knowledge better than his appearance. He said: I have appointed you to such and such of my work. He asked to be excused, but he refused to excuse him. He said: O governor, shall I not inform you of something my father told me that he heard from the Messenger of Allah, may Allah bless him and grant him peace? He said: Bring it. He said: He heard the Prophet, may Allah bless him and grant him peace, say: 'Whoever takes a position while knowing that he is not fit for that position, then let him take his seat in Hell.' And I testify, O governor, that I am not fit for what you have called me to. Yazid said to him: You have only increased our desire for you and made us wish for you more. Go to your appointment, for I will not excuse you. So he left and stayed in it as long as Allah willed. Then he asked permission to come to him, and he gave him permission. He said to him: O governor, shall I not relate to you something my father told him that he heard from the Messenger of Allah, may Allah bless him and grant him peace? He said: Bring it. He said: 'Cursed is the one who asks, and cursed is the one who is asked in the name of Allah and then withholds from the asker what he did not ask for [as a gift].' I ask you by Allah except you excuse me, O governor, from your work. So he excused him.
Source pages p. 77

Commentary
This passage is a biographical entry from Ibn Asakir's Tabyin Kadhib al-Muftari. It focuses on Abu Burda ibn Abi Musa al-Ash'ari, the son of the famous companion Abu Musa al-Ash'ari. The author begins by stating that he will now mention Abu Burda's name and virtues. The first narration establishes his name as 'Amir ibn Abdullah ibn Qays, citing multiple chains including al-Bukhari. It also reports that he was the judge of Kufa and was dismissed by al-Hajjaj, who appointed his brother in his place. His age is given as eighty years through a report from Umar ibn Abd al-Aziz. The text then notes a disagreement: although most scholars agree his name is 'Amir, Yahya ibn Ma'in reports that it may be al-Harith, and some combine both. Another narration from al-Hajjaj's time confirms his appointment with Sa'id ibn Jubayr as scribe, and his trustworthiness (thiqah) is affirmed. The final and longest narration tells a story demonstrating Abu Burda's piety. When Yazid ibn al-Muhallab wanted to appoint him to a position in Khurasan, Abu Burda first cited a hadith warning against taking a post when unfit, but Yazid insisted. Later, Abu Burda cited another hadith about the curse on one who asks in Allah's name and refuses, thereby pleading for exemption. Yazid then excused him. This story highlights Abu Burda's scrupulousness in avoiding positions of authority unless qualified, following the prophetic warnings.

أبو بردة
أبو موسى الأشعري
الحجاج
القضاء
عامر بن عبد الله بن قيس

What was Abu Burda's official position in Kufa?
A) Governor of Kufa
B) Judge of Kufa
C) Treasurer of Kufa
D) Military commander

Key Takeaway
Abu Burda was a trustworthy Kufan judge whose name is predominantly 'Amir, with a rare variant of al-Harith.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
How meticulously do you verify the names and details of Islamic scholars before quoting them? Could you bear being the source of a misattributed fact that later generations rely on? Commit to checking at least two sources before transmitting a name.
?
Abu Burda remained trustworthy even while serving under a controversial ruler. When you face pressure to compromise your integrity in a difficult environment, what will you hold onto? Reflect on a specific situation where you might be tempted to bend the truth and plan how to remain upright.
?
The scholars preserved both the dominant name and a rare variant for completeness. Are there truths in your life—inconvenient or rare—that you have buried? Pledge to acknowledge them honestly, even if they challenge your comfort or reputation.

Life of Imam Ibn Asakir
beginner

Abu Burda's Refusal of Office: Prophetic Warnings
Ibn Asakir narrates Abu Burda's encounter with Yazid ibn al-Muhallab, where he cites two hadiths to be excused from a governorship.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Abu Burda used two prophetic hadiths to avoid a governorship, demonstrating the importance of competency and piety in leadership.

Core Idea
Abu Burda refused a governorship by citing hadiths warning against taking leadership while unqualified and against refusing a request made in Allah's name.

Ibn Asakir concludes the biography of Abu Burda with a powerful story that illustrates his piety. When Yazid ibn al-Muhallab appointed him to a position in Khurasan, Abu Burda did not simply refuse; he cited prophetic warnings to persuade the governor. This story highlights the high standard of responsibility in Islamic leadership.

Guided Path
1
Read the story and identify the two hadiths
Read the Arabic and translation. Note the two hadiths Abu Burda cites: one about taking a position while unfit, and the other about the curse on the one who asks in Allah's name and then refuses.
Pay attention to how he applies each hadith to his situation.
2
Analyze the dialogue between Abu Burda and Yazid
Observe the exchange: Yazid insists after the first hadith, but Abu Burda uses the second hadith to finally gain exemption. Consider why the second hadith worked.
3
Reflect on the lesson for leadership
Think about the implications: Abu Burda's refusal was not based on laziness but on fear of accountability and adherence to prophetic teachings. This sets a precedent for avoiding positions one is not qualified for.
This connects to the common misunderstanding that he was avoiding work.

As for the mention of his son Abu Burda, his name and virtue, part of it is what the two shaykhs informed us: Abu al-Hasan Ali ibn Hibbat Allah ibn Abd al-Salam al-Katib al-Baghdadi and Abu al-Qasim ibn al-Samarqandi, they said: Abu Bakr ibn Abdullah ibn Muhammad al-Sarifini narrated to us: Ubayd Allah ibn Muhammad ibn Ishaq al-Bazzaz narrated to us: Abdullah ibn Muhammad al-Baghawi narrated to us, he said: The name of Abu Burda is 'Amir ibn Abdullah ibn Qays. Mahmud ibn Ghaylan said that. Also, Salih ibn Ahmad (meaning ibn Hanbal) narrated to me from his father. And the shaykh Abu Abdullah al-Farawi informed us: Abu Bakr al-Bayhaqi narrated to us: Muhammad ibn Ibrahim narrated to us: Ibrahim ibn Abdullah narrated to us: Muhammad ibn Sulayman ibn Faris narrated to us: Muhammad ibn Isma'il al-Bukhari narrated to us, he said: 'Amir ibn Abdullah ibn Qays is Abu Burda ibn Abi Musa al-Ash'ari, may Allah be pleased with him. Amr ibn Ali narrated to me from Abu Dawud from Sulayman ibn Mu'adh from Abu Ishaq, he said: Abu Burda ibn Abi Musa was in charge of the judiciary of Kufa, then al-Hajjaj dismissed him and appointed his brother in his place. He heard from his father, Ali, and Ibn Umar. Ali said: I heard Sufyan say: Umar ibn Abd al-Aziz said to Abu Burda: How old are you? He said: A shaddan, meaning eighty years. And Abu Bakr Muhammad ibn al-Abbas al-Shiqani informed us: Abu Bakr Ahmad ibn Mansur ibn Khalaf al-Qayrawani narrated to us: Abu Sa'id Muhammad ibn Abdullah ibn Hamdawayh narrated to us: Abu Hatim Makki ibn Abdan informed us, he said: I heard Abu al-Husayn Muslim ibn al-Hajjaj al-Qushayri al-Hafiz say: Abu Burda ibn Abi Musa al-Ash'ari is 'Amir ibn Abdullah ibn Qays. He heard from his father and Ali. Al-Sha'bi and Abu Ishaq narrated from him. Most of the memorizers agreed that the name of Abu Burda is 'Amir. Yahya ibn Ma'in said a rare statement about his name. The shaykh Abu Abdullah Muhammad ibn al-Fadl informed us: Ahmad ibn al-Husayn al-Hafiz narrated to us: Abdullah ibn Yahya ibn Abd al-Jabbar al-Sukkari in Baghdad narrated to us: Abu Bakr al-Shafi'i narrated to us: Ja'far ibn Muhammad ibn al-Azhar narrated to us: al-Mufaddal ibn Ghassan al-Ghalabi narrated to us from someone who said: Abu Burda ibn Abi Musa's name is al-Harith. And Abbas ibn Muhammad al-Dawri reported from Yahya ibn Ma'in that he named him with both names, and Abbas mentioned him in his history in two places. And the shaykh Abu al-Fadl Muhammad ibn Isma'il al-Fadili al-Hirawi informed us: Abu al-Qasim Ahmad ibn Muhammad ibn Muhammad al-Khalili in Balkh narrated to us: Abu al-Qasim Ali ibn Ahmad ibn Muhammad ibn al-Hasan al-Khuza'i narrated to us: Abu Sa'id al-Haytham ibn Kulayb al-Shashi narrated to us: Abu Qilaba Abd al-Malik ibn Muhammad al-Raqqashi narrated to us: Raja' ibn Salama ibn Raja narrated to me: my father narrated to me: Qays ibn al-Rabi' narrated to us from Abu Husayn, he said: When al-Hajjaj came to Iraq, he appointed Abd al-Rahman ibn Abi Layla as judge. He said: Then he dismissed him and appointed Abu Burda ibn Abi Musa, and seated Sa'id ibn Jubayr with him. The shaykh Abu al-Barakat Abd al-Wahhab ibn al-Mubarak al-Anmati informed us: Abu al-Husayn al-Mubarak ibn Abd al-Jabbar ibn Ahmad narrated to us: Abu Abdullah al-Husayn ibn Ja'far ibn Muhammad al-Salamsi and his cousin Abu Nasr Muhammad ibn al-Hasan ibn Muhammad [said?] – and the shaykh Abu Abdullah al-Husayn ibn Muhammad ibn Khusraw al-Balkhi in Baghdad informed us: Abu al-Ma'ali Thabit ibn Bundar ibn Ibrahim narrated to us: al-Husayn ibn Ja'far al-Salamsi narrated to us, they said: al-Walid ibn Bakr al-Andalusi narrated to us: Ali ibn Ahmad ibn Zakariyya al-Hashimi narrated to us: Abu Muslim Salih ibn Abdullah ibn Salih al-Ijli narrated to us, he said: My father said: Abu Burda ibn Abi Musa al-Ash'ari is a Kufan, trustworthy, and he was in charge of the judiciary of Kufa after Shurayh, and his scribe was Sa'id ibn Jubayr. The shaykh Abu Sahl Muhammad ibn Ibrahim ibn Muhammad al-Isbahani informed us: Abu al-Fadl Abd al-Rahman ibn Ahmad ibn al-Hasan al-Razi narrated to us: Ja'far ibn Abdullah ibn Ya'qub narrated to us: Muhammad ibn Harun al-Ruwyani narrated to us: Ahmad ibn Abd al-Rahman narrated to us: my uncle, meaning Abdullah ibn Wahab, told me: Abdullah ibn Ayyash narrated to me from his father that when Yazid ibn al-Muhallab became governor of Khurasan, he said: Direct me to a man complete in good qualities. So he was directed to Abu Burda ibn Abi Musa al-Ash'ari. When he came to him, he saw an excellent man. When he spoke to him, he found his inner knowledge better than his appearance. He said: I have appointed you to such and such of my work. He asked to be excused, but he refused to excuse him. He said: O governor, shall I not inform you of something my father told me that he heard from the Messenger of Allah, may Allah bless him and grant him peace? He said: Bring it. He said: He heard the Prophet, may Allah bless him and grant him peace, say: 'Whoever takes a position while knowing that he is not fit for that position, then let him take his seat in Hell.' And I testify, O governor, that I am not fit for what you have called me to. Yazid said to him: You have only increased our desire for you and made us wish for you more. Go to your appointment, for I will not excuse you. So he left and stayed in it as long as Allah willed. Then he asked permission to come to him, and he gave him permission. He said to him: O governor, shall I not relate to you something my father told him that he heard from the Messenger of Allah, may Allah bless him and grant him peace? He said: Bring it. He said: 'Cursed is the one who asks, and cursed is the one who is asked in the name of Allah and then withholds from the asker what he did not ask for [as a gift].' I ask you by Allah except you excuse me, O governor, from your work. So he excused him.
Source pages p. 77

Commentary
This passage is a biographical entry from Ibn Asakir's Tabyin Kadhib al-Muftari. It focuses on Abu Burda ibn Abi Musa al-Ash'ari, the son of the famous companion Abu Musa al-Ash'ari. The author begins by stating that he will now mention Abu Burda's name and virtues. The first narration establishes his name as 'Amir ibn Abdullah ibn Qays, citing multiple chains including al-Bukhari. It also reports that he was the judge of Kufa and was dismissed by al-Hajjaj, who appointed his brother in his place. His age is given as eighty years through a report from Umar ibn Abd al-Aziz. The text then notes a disagreement: although most scholars agree his name is 'Amir, Yahya ibn Ma'in reports that it may be al-Harith, and some combine both. Another narration from al-Hajjaj's time confirms his appointment with Sa'id ibn Jubayr as scribe, and his trustworthiness (thiqah) is affirmed. The final and longest narration tells a story demonstrating Abu Burda's piety. When Yazid ibn al-Muhallab wanted to appoint him to a position in Khurasan, Abu Burda first cited a hadith warning against taking a post when unfit, but Yazid insisted. Later, Abu Burda cited another hadith about the curse on one who asks in Allah's name and refuses, thereby pleading for exemption. Yazid then excused him. This story highlights Abu Burda's scrupulousness in avoiding positions of authority unless qualified, following the prophetic warnings.

Key Terms
يزيد بن المهلب
خراسان
تولى عملا
غير أهل
ملعون من سأل بوجه الله

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think Abu Burda refused all positions out of laziness or desire to avoid responsibility.
Correction: The hadiths show he refused due to fear of being unqualified and accountability before Allah, not laziness. He actually served temporarily when pressured.

Checkpoint
What did Abu Burda say about the second hadith that finally convinced Yazid to excuse him?
A) He cited a hadith about the punishment for unjust rulers.
B) He cited a hadith about the curse on the one who asks and the one who withholds in Allah's name.
C) He cited a hadith about the reward for those who avoid leadership.
D) He cited a hadith about the obligation to obey the ruler.

Key Takeaway
Abu Burda used prophetic warnings to avoid a position he felt unqualified for, demonstrating that leadership requires competence and that refusing such positions can be an act of piety.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When was the last time you took on a responsibility you knew you were not qualified for? What did you tell yourself to justify it, and what does that reveal about your fear of accountability before Allah?
?
Abu Burda used a hadith about being asked in Allah's name to escape a difficult situation. Have you ever used Allah's name to get what you want or to manipulate someone's decision? How can you repent from that and practice sincerity in your requests?
?
If you were offered a leadership role you felt unqualified for, would you have the courage to say no as Abu Burda did, or would you fear disappointing others? What does your answer say about your priorities between pleasing people and pleasing Allah?

Life of Imam Ibn Asakir
beginner
4 min
Abu Burda's Refusal Based on Qualification
Abu Burda's appointment and refusal using hadith on qualification

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that Islamic leadership requires competence and that accepting a role without qualification incurs a divine warning.

Core Idea
Competence is a condition for leadership; pretending otherwise invites Hellfire.

Abu Burda ibn Abi Musa, a respected early judge, was offered a governorship by Yazid ibn al-Muhallab. Rather than accept out of ambition, he cited a prophetic warning against undertaking work for which one is unqualified. This lesson explores that hadith and its implication for leadership.

Guided Path
1
Read the narrative of Abu Burda's appointment and first refusal
Read the Arabic and translation carefully to see how Abu Burda uses the Prophet’s words. Pay attention to his honesty about his qualifications.
2
Identify the hadith about qualification
Find the exact wording of the hadith and notice the severe warning: 'let him take his seat in the Fire.'
Arabic: 'فليتبوا مقعده من النار'
3
Examine Yazid's response
Notice that Yazid only increased his desire for Abu Burda, showing that humility and honesty are admired, though he still insisted.
4
Reflect on the principle
Consider how this applies to modern leadership roles: acceptance of a role without competence is a religious violation.
This is not mere humility but a religious obligation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
احمد ابن سلمة بن رجاء حدثني أبي نا قيس بن الربيع عن أبي حصين قال لما قدم الحجاج العراق استعمل عبد الرحمن بن أبي ليلى على القضاء قال ثم عزله واستعمل ابا بردة بن أبي موسى وأقعد معه سعيد بن جبير، أخبرنا الشيخ أبو البركات عبد الوهاب بن المبارك الانماطى انا أبو الحسين المبارك بن عبد الجبار بن احمد انا أبو عبد الله الحسين بن جعفر بن محمد السلماسی وابن عمه أبو نصر محمد بن الحسن بن محمد خ وأخبرنا الشيخ أبو عبد الله الحسين بن محمد بن خسرو البلخی ببغداد انا أبو المعالي ثابت بن بندار بن ابراهيم انا الحسين بن جعفر السلماسي قالا انا الوليد بن بكر الاندلسی نا علی بن احمد بن زكريا الهاشمى نا أبو مسلم صالح بن بن عبد الله بن صالح العجلي قال قال ابى : أبو بردة بن أبي موسى الأشعرى كوفى ثقة وكان على قضاء الكوفة ولى بعد شريح وكان كاتبه سعید بن جبير، أخبرنا الشيخ أبو سهل محمد بن ابراهيم بن محمد الأصبهاني انا أبو الفضل عبدالرحمن بن احمد بن الحسن الرازي انا جعفر ابن عبد الله بن يعقوب نا محمد بن هرون الروياني انا احمد بن عبدالرحمن نا عمى یعنی عبدالله بن هب حدثني عبدالله بن عياش عن ابيه ان يزيد ابن المهلب لما ولى خراسان قال دلوني على رجل كامل الخصال الخير فدل على أبي بردة بن أبي موسى الأشعرى فلما جاءه راه رجلا فائقا فلما كلمه رأی مخبرته أفضل من مرانه قال انى وليتك كذا وكذا من عملى فاستعفاه فأبى ان يعقيه فقال ايها الامير ألا أخبرك بشيء حدثنيه أبى انه سمعه من رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم قال هاته قال انه سمع النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم يقول ( من تولى عملا وهو يعلم انه ليس لذلك العمل بأهل فليتبوا مقعده من النار وانا اشهد ايها الامير اني لست بأهل لما دعوتني اليه فقال له يزيد مازدت على ان حرضتنى على نفسك ورغبنا فيك فاخرج الى عهدك فاني غير معفيك فخرج ثم اقام فيه ماشاء الله ان يقيم فاستأذنه بالقدوم عليه فأذن له فقال له أيها الأمير ألا أحدثك بشيء حدثنيه أبى انه سمعه من رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم قال هاته قال ( ملعون من سأل وملعون من سئل بوجه الله ثم منع سائله ما لم يسله هجرا وانا الله الا ما اعفيتنى ايها الأمير من عملك ) فأعفاه .
Ahmad ibn Salamah ibn Raja' told us: my father narrated to us: Qays ibn al-Rabi' narrated from Abu Husayn who said: When al-Hajjaj came to Iraq, he appointed 'Abd al-Rahman ibn Abi Layla to the judiciary, then he dismissed him and appointed Abu Burda ibn Abi Musa, and seated with him Sa'id ibn Jubayr. (Chain) al-'Ajli said: my father said: Abu Burda ibn Abi Musa al-Ash'ari is a reliable Kufan, he was over the judiciary of Kufa after Shurayh, and his scribe was Sa'id ibn Jubayr. (Chain) from 'Abdullah ibn 'Ayyash from his father that when Yazid ibn al-Muhallab was appointed over Khurasan, he said: guide me to a man perfect in good qualities, so he was guided to Abu Burda ibn Abi Musa al-Ash'ari. When he came, he saw a man of outstanding appearance, and when he spoke, he found his inner quality better than his appearance. He said: I have appointed you to such-and-such of my duties. He asked to be excused, so he refused to excuse him. He said: O commander, shall I not inform you of something my father told me he heard from the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him)? He said: bring it. He said: he heard the Prophet (peace be upon him) say: 'Whoever undertakes a work while knowing that he is not qualified for that work, let him take his seat in the Fire. And I testify, O commander, that I am not qualified for what you have called me to.' Yazid said to him: you have only increased my desire for you and our desire in you. Go out to your appointment, for I will not excuse you. So he went out and stayed in it as long as Allah willed. Then he asked permission to come to him, and he permitted him. He said: O commander, shall I not inform you of something my father told me he heard from the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him)? He said: bring it. He said: 'Cursed is the one who asks, and cursed is the one who is asked by the face of Allah and then refuses his asker, unless he asks for something sinful. I ask you by Allah, O commander, except you excuse me from your work.' So he excused him.
Source pages p. 78

Commentary
This passage is a biographical report about Abu Burda ibn Abi Musa al-Ash'ari, a prominent early judge and narrator. It begins with a chain tracing back to Abu Husayn, who reports that when al-Hajjaj came to Iraq, he first appointed 'Abd al-Rahman ibn Abi Layla as judge, then replaced him with Abu Burda, with Sa'id ibn Jubayr as his scribe. This establishes Abu Burda's early judicial role. Next, a chain from al-'Ajli confirms Abu Burda's reliability (thiqah) and notes he was judge of Kufa after Shurayh. The core of the passage is a story from Yazid ibn al-Muhallab, who sought a man of perfect qualities for a governorship in Khurasan. He found Abu Burda, who impressed him both in appearance and speech. Yazid appointed him, but Abu Burda asked to be excused. When Yazid refused, Abu Burda cited a hadith from his father Abu Musa: 'Whoever undertakes a work while knowing he is not qualified for it, let him take his seat in the Fire.' He used this to argue he was not qualified. Yazid was even more impressed but insisted. Later, Abu Burda returned and cited another hadith: 'Cursed is the one who asks, and cursed is the one who is asked by the face of Allah and then refuses, unless it is a sinful request.' He begged to be excused by the face of Allah, and Yazid finally excused him. The passage highlights Abu Burda's scrupulousness and the principle of only accepting positions for which one is qualified.

Key Terms
أبو بردة بن أبي موسى
تولية العمل
حديث من تولى عملا

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That Abu Burda refused the position out of personal humility only; the hadith shows it is a religious obligation not to take a role one is unqualified for.
Correction: The hadith makes it a matter of sin, not mere humility.

Checkpoint
According to the hadith cited by Abu Burda, what is the consequence for someone who undertakes a work knowing they are not qualified?
A) They are cursed
B) They will take their seat in the Fire
C) They will be forgiven
D) They will be praised

Key Takeaway
Abu Burda's refusal shows that knowingly accepting a position for which one is unqualified is a religious violation with severe consequences.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever accepted a responsibility—at work, in your family, or in your community—while knowing deep down that you were not truly qualified or prepared? What excuses did you make to yourself, and how does Abu Burda's refusal challenge those excuses?
?
The Prophet ﷺ said that those who knowingly take on work they are not qualified for will take their seat in the Fire. Is there any role you currently hold that you are not competent for? What would it cost you to step down or seek help, and what is the cost of staying?
?
Abu Burda's honesty impressed the governor even as he refused. How often do you prioritize people's approval over your own accountability to Allah? Will you commit today to never again say 'yes' to a role you are not fit for, no matter how noble the cause?

Abu Burda's Second Hadith and Release from Duty
Abu Burda's second hadith asking by Allah's face to be excused

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the prohibition of asking in Allah's name and then refusing, as demonstrated by Abu Burda's successful plea.

Core Idea
Asking by Allah's face is sacred; refusing such a request incurs a curse.

After reluctantly accepting the post, Abu Burda later returned to Yazid and cited another hadith from the Prophet about the curse on those who ask by Allah's face and then are refused. Using this, he finally persuaded Yazid to excuse him. This lesson explores that second hadith and its application.

Guided Path
1
Read the continuation of the story
Read from 'فخرج ثم اقام' to the end. Notice the second hadith and how Abu Burda applies it.
2
Analyze the second hadith
U'nderstand the warning: 'Cursed is the one who asks, and cursed is the one who is asked by the face of Allah and then refuses, unless the request is sinful.'
The exception is key: only sinful requests can be refused.
3
See how Abu Burda applies it
He begs by Allah to be excused, using the threat of the curse to persuade Yazid.
This shows practical use of religious teachings.
4
Observe the outcome
Yazid finally excuses him, showing the power of the hadith and the respect for such a plea.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That asking by Allah's face is always permissible without consequence; the hadith prohibits both asking and refusing in that manner.

?

?

?
Abu Burda used this hadith to be excused from a role he was not qualified for. Are you currently holding a position or avoiding a duty using religious justifications? What would integrity demand of you?

Bilal ibn Abi Burda: Judge of Basra and Stance Against Heretics
Multiple chains confirm Bilal's identity and his strictness against heretics.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the biographical details of Bilal ibn Abi Burda, his appointment as judge of Basra, and his notable severity against the 'people of desires' (ahl al-ahwa').

Core Idea
Bilal's strictness against heretics defined his legacy and affected his descendants.

This passage from Ibn 'Asakir's work uses multiple chains of transmission to establish the identity and official roles of Bilal ibn Abi Burda, a judge in Basra. It also highlights a key trait: his harsh treatment of those deemed heretical, a stance that had repercussions for his family. As you read, notice how the biographical details are reinforced through different isnads, and consider why the author might emphasize Bilal's severity.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once aloud, noting the multiple chains. Then read the literal translation to grasp the flow of information.
2
Identify the key biographical facts
From the passage, list what we learn about Bilal: his full name, kunya, father's name, his position, and who he heard from. Notice how each chain adds a piece of information.
The chains from al-Nasa'i, al-Bukhari, and al-Asma'i all contribute.
3
Focus on the historical account
The final chain describes the political context: Khalid al-Qasri's appointments. Bilal is put in charge of police, prayer, and judiciary in Basra after Malik ibn al-Mundhir is dismissed.
4
Analyze the concluding statement
The passage ends by noting Bilal's severity against ahl al-ahwa' and that this left consequences for his descendants. Ask yourself: what does this imply about the political and social tensions of the time?
This is a key point for understanding the sectarian dimension.

وأما ابنه بلال بن أبي بردة أخبرنا الشيخ أبو الفضل محمد بن ناصر احمد بن محمد الحافظ ببغداد انا القاضى أبو الفضل جعفر بن يحي بن ابراهيم التميمي المكي المعروف بالحكاك اجازة ان لم اكن سمعته منه انا أبو نصر بن سعيد بن حاتم بن احمد الوائلي السجستاني انا القاضي أبو الحسن الخصيب بن عبدالله بن محمد بن محمد بن الخصيب اخبرني أبو عبدالرحمن النسائي أخبرني أبو موسى عبد الكريم بن أحمد بن شعيب بن علی النسائي قال أبو عمرو بلال بن أبي بردة بن أبي موسى الأشعرى، وأخبرنا الشيخ الفقيه أبو الفتح نصر الله بن محمد بن عبدالقوى المصيصي انا أبو الفتح نصر بن ابراهيم بن نصر المقدسي الفقيه بصور انا أبو الفتح سليم بن ايوب ابن سليم الرازي الفقيه انا أبو نصر طاهر بن محمد بن سليمان بن يوسف الموصلى بالموصل نا أبو القسم على ابن ابراهيم بن احمد الجوزی نا أبو زكريا يزيد بن محمد بن اياس قال سمعت القاضي محمد بن ابن محمد بن أبى بكر المقدمى يقول بلال ابن أبي بردة ابن أبي موسى الأشعرى يكنى أبا عبد الله وأبو بردة اسمه عامر؛ وأخبرنا الشيخ أبو عبدالله محمد بن الفضل الفقيه انا أبو بكر احمد بن الحسين البيهقي، انا محمد ابن ابراهيم الفارسي انا ابراهيم بن عبد الله نا محمد بن سليمان بن فارس نا محمد بن اسماعيل البخارى رحمه الله قال بلال بن أبي بردة بن أبي موسى الأشعرى قاضى البصرة سمع اباه روى عنه قتادة وهو اخو سعيد بن عامر بن عبد الله بن قيس، وأخبرنا الشيخ أبو القسم اسماعيل بن احمد بن السمر قندى انا أبو الحسين احمد بن محمد بن النقور، والقاضي أبو منصور عبد الباقي بن محمد ابن غالب بن العطار قالا انا أبو طاهر محمد ابن عبدالرحمن بن العباس المخلص انا أبو محمد عبيد الله بن عبدالرحمن ابن عيسى السكرى نا أبو يعلى زكريا بن يحيى النقرى، نا الأصمعي نا بن بلال عن مجالد قال ثم ولى العراق خالد بن عبد الله القسري فكان على شرطته بواسط عمرو ابن عبد الأعلى الحكمي، واستعمل على الكوفة العريان بن الهيثم واستعمل على البصرة مالك بن المنذر بن الجارود العبدى ثم عزله واستعمل بلال بن أبي بردة فكان على الأحداث والصلاة والقضاء وكان بلال بن أبي بردة شديدا على أهل الأهواء فأورث ذلك عقبه
As for his son Bilal ibn Abi Burda: (Chain) Abu 'Amr said: Bilal ibn Abi Burda ibn Abi Musa al-Ash'ari. (Another chain) I heard the judge Muhammad ibn Muhammad ibn Abi Bakr al-Muqaddami say: Bilal ibn Abi Burda ibn Abi Musa al-Ash'ari is nicknamed Abu 'Abdullah, and Abu Burda's name is 'Amir. (Chain) al-Bukhari said: Bilal ibn Abi Burda ibn Abi Musa al-Ash'ari, judge of Basra, heard from his father, and Qatadah narrated from him, and he is the brother of Sa'id ibn 'Amir ibn 'Abdullah ibn Qays. (Chain) from al-Asma'i from ibn Bilal from Mujalid who said: Then Khalid ibn 'Abdullah al-Qasri was put in charge of Iraq, and his police chief in Wasit was 'Amr ibn 'Abd al-A'la al-Hakami. He appointed over Kufa al-'Uryan ibn al-Haytham, and over Basra Malik ibn al-Mundhir ibn al-Jarud al-'Abdi, then he dismissed him and appointed Bilal ibn Abi Burda, who was in charge of police, prayer, and judiciary. Bilal ibn Abi Burda was severe against the people of desires (ahl al-ahwa'), and that left consequences for his descendants.
Source pages p. 79

Commentary
This passage is a biographical collection about Bilal ibn Abi Burda, son of the previously mentioned Abu Burda. It comprises multiple chains (isnads) that establish his identity, his nickname (kunya), his father's name, and his role. The first chain from al-Nasa'i simply states his name. The second chain adds that his kunya is Abu 'Abdullah and his father's name is 'Amir. The third chain from al-Bukhari confirms he was the judge of Basra, heard from his father, had Qatadah as a narrator from him, and identifies his brother Sa'id. The fourth chain, through al-Asma'i, provides historical context: when Khalid al-Qasri ruled Iraq, he appointed various officials. He first appointed Malik ibn al-Mundhir over Basra, then dismissed him and appointed Bilal ibn Abi Burda. Bilal was put in charge of the police (al-ahdath), prayer, and judiciary. The passage concludes with an important note: Bilal was severe against 'ahl al-ahwa'' (people of heretical desires, often referring to innovators or sectarians), and this severity left a lasting effect on his descendants. This detail shows that his strict stance had repercussions for his family, possibly due to enmity from those sects.

Key Terms
بلال بن أبي بردة
قاضي البصرة
أهل الأهواء
خالد بن عبد الله القسري
الأحداث

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What position did Bilal ibn Abi Burda hold in Basra under Khalid al-Qasri?
A) Governor of Basra
B) Judge of Basra and in charge of police and prayer
C) Chief of police only
D) Tax collector

Key Takeaway
Bilal ibn Abi Burda was a judge of Basra known for his strictness against heretics, a trait that left a lasting impact on his descendants.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you see someone with a belief you consider wrong, what is your immediate reaction? Is it severity like Bilal's, or something else? What would your family say about how you treat others who disagree with you?
?
Bilal's severity left 'consequences for his descendants.' What kind of spiritual or relational legacy are you building today through your attitudes toward those who differ from you—especially in matters of faith?
?
If you were in a position of authority (as a parent, teacher, or leader), how would you balance standing firm on truth with showing mercy? Can you name one concrete change you need to make this week in how you deal with someone who holds a view you oppose?

Biography of Bilal ibn Abi Burda
Ibn Asakir transmits multiple chains establishing Bilal's biography.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how classical Islamic biographical entries are constructed through multiple chains of transmission and what key details are established about Bilal ibn Abi Burda.

Core Idea
Multiple chains of transmission authenticate the biography of a key early figure.

Ibn Asakir presents several chains of narration to establish the biography of Bilal ibn Abi Burda, the grandson of the famous companion Abu Musa al-Ash'ari. These chains not only confirm his identity but also highlight his role as judge of Basra. This lesson explores the structure of such biographical entries.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the full translation to understand the content.
Notice the repetition of similar information in different chains.
2
Identify the three main chains
The passage contains three distinct chains of transmission. Find where each chain begins and trace the narrators down to the final statement.
The first chain ends with al-Nasa'i, the second with al-Muqaddami, the third with al-Bukhari.
3
Note the key biographical facts
Identify Bilal's full name, his kunya, his father's name, his position, his teacher, the one who narrated from him, and his family relation.
These facts are spread across the chains and confirmed by multiple sources.
4
Reflect on the purpose of multiple chains
Ask yourself why the author includes multiple transmissions. How does this strengthen the biography?
This reinforces the reliability of the information.

وأما ابنه بلال بن أبي بردة أخبرنا الشيخ أبو الفضل محمد بن ناصر الحافظ ببغداد أنا القاضي أبو الفضل جعفر بن يحيى بن إبراهيم التميمي المكي المعروف بالحكاك إجازة إن لم أكن سمعته منه أنا أبو نصر بن سعيد بن حاتم بن أحمد الوائلي السجستاني أنا القاضي أبو الحسن الخصيب بن عبد الله بن محمد بن محمد بن الخصيب أخبرني أبو عبد الرحمن النسائي أخبرني أبو موسى عبد الكريم بن أحمد بن شعيب بن علي النسائي قال أبو عمرو بلال بن أبي بردة بن أبي موسى الأشعري. وأخبرنا الشيخ الفقيه أبو الفتح نصر الله بن محمد بن عبد القوي المصيصي أنا أبو الفتح نصر بن إبراهيم بن نصر المقدسي الفقيه بصور أنا أبو الفتح سليم بن أيوب بن سليم الرازي الفقيه أنا أبو نصر طاهر بن محمد بن سليمان بن يوسف الموصلي بالموصل نا أبو القاسم علي بن إبراهيم بن أحمد الجوزي نا أبو زكريا يزيد بن محمد بن إياس قال سمعت القاضي محمد بن أبي بكر المقدمي يقول بلال بن أبي بردة بن أبي موسى الأشعري يكنى أبا عبد الله وأبو بردة اسمه عامر. وأخبرنا الشيخ أبو عبد الله محمد بن الفضل الفقيه أنا أبو بكر أحمد بن الحسين البيهقي أنا محمد بن إبراهيم الفارسي أنا إبراهيم بن عبد الله نا محمد بن سليمان بن فارس نا محمد بن إسماعيل البخاري رحمه الله قال بلال بن أبي بردة بن أبي موسى الأشعري قاضي البصرة سمع أباه روى عنه قتادة وهو أخو سعيد بن عامر بن عبد الله بن قيس.
As for his son, Bilal ibn Abi Burda: The shaykh Abu al-Fadl Muhammad ibn Nasir al-Hafiz in Baghdad informed us, saying: al-Qadi Abu al-Fadl Ja'far ibn Yahya ibn Ibrahim al-Tamimi al-Makki known as al-Hakkak informed us by permission if I did not hear it from him, saying: Abu Nasr ibn Sa'id ibn Hatim ibn Ahmad al-Wa'ili al-Sijistani informed us, saying: al-Qadi Abu al-Hasan al-Khasib ibn 'Abd Allah ibn Muhammad ibn Muhammad ibn al-Khasib informed us, saying: Abu 'Abd al-Rahman al-Nasa'i informed me, saying: Abu Musa 'Abd al-Karim ibn Ahmad ibn Shu'ayb ibn 'Ali al-Nasa'i informed me, saying: Abu 'Amr Bilal ibn Abi Burda ibn Abi Musa al-Ash'ari. And the shaykh al-faqih Abu al-Fath Nasr Allah ibn Muhammad ibn 'Abd al-Qawi al-Masisi informed us, saying: Abu al-Fath Nasr ibn Ibrahim ibn Nasr al-Maqdisi al-faqih in Sur informed us, saying: Abu al-Fath Sulaym ibn Ayyub ibn Sulaym al-Razi al-faqih informed us, saying: Abu Nasr Tahir ibn Muhammad ibn Sulayman ibn Yusuf al-Mawsili in Mosul narrated to us, saying: Abu al-Qasim 'Ali ibn Ibrahim ibn Ahmad al-Jawzi narrated to us, saying: Abu Zakariyya Yazid ibn Muhammad ibn Iyas narrated to us, saying: I heard al-Qadi Muhammad ibn Abi Bakr al-Muqaddami say: Bilal ibn Abi Burda ibn Abi Musa al-Ash'ari is nicknamed Abu 'Abd Allah, and Abu Burda's name is 'Amir. And the shaykh Abu 'Abd Allah Muhammad ibn al-Fadl al-faqih informed us, saying: Abu Bakr Ahmad ibn al-Husayn al-Bayhaqi informed us, saying: Muhammad ibn Ibrahim al-Farisi informed us, saying: Ibrahim ibn 'Abd Allah narrated to us, saying: Muhammad ibn Sulayman ibn Faris narrated to us, saying: Muhammad ibn Isma'il al-Bukhari, may Allah have mercy on him, said: Bilal ibn Abi Burda ibn Abi Musa al-Ash'ari was the judge of Basra; he heard from his father; Qatada narrated from him; and he is the brother of Sa'id ibn 'Amir ibn 'Abd Allah ibn Qays.
Source pages p. 79

Commentary
This passage provides multiple chains of transmission establishing the biography of Bilal ibn Abi Burda, the grandson of Abu Musa al-Ash'ari. The author, Ibn 'Asakir, begins by citing a chain through al-Hafiz Abu al-Fadl in Baghdad, then through al-Qadi al-Hakka, and so on down to al-Nasa'i, who records that Bilal is known as Abu 'Amr. Another chain through al-Masisi and al-Maqdisi includes a statement from al-Qadi Muhammad ibn Abi Bakr al-Muqaddami that Bilal's kunya is Abu 'Abd Allah, and that his father Abu Burda's name is 'Amir. Finally, a chain through al-Bayhaqi and al-Bukhari confirms that Bilal was the judge of Basra, heard from his father, that Qatada narrated from him, and that he was the brother of Sa'id ibn 'Amir ibn 'Abd Allah ibn Qays. The chains serve to authenticate the biographical details, emphasizing Bilal's noble lineage and position.

Key Terms
بلال بن أبي بردة
أبو موسى الأشعري
قاضي البصرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Multiple chains in biographical entries are redundant repetitions.
Correction: Each chain independently reinforces the reliability of the information, as seen in the three distinct chains attesting to Bilal's identity and position.

Checkpoint

C) Commander of the faithful
D) Scholar of Medina

Key Takeaway
Bilal ibn Abi Burda was a judge of Basra, grandson of Abu Musa al-Ash'ari, and his biography is authenticated by multiple independent chains.
